The Core Ruling: Dependent's Intention Subservient to the Master's
Commentary on the subservience of the dependent's intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the dependent's prayer length follows the master's intention, and explain why knowledge of that intention is required for the ruling to take effect.

Core Idea
The dependent's intention is ignored; only the master's counts, but mercy requires the dependent knows before being bound.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the ruling in Kanz al-Daqa'iq that in travel, the intention of residence or travel is taken from the original (master, husband, commander), not from the dependent (woman, slave, soldier). The commentator clarifies the underlying principle: the dependent is not required to complete prayer until he or she actually knows of the master's intention, because imposing a ruling without knowledge causes hardship. This lesson unpacks that core ruling and the intra-Hanafi debate around it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main legal rule
From the text: 'And the intention of residence and travel is considered from the original, not from the dependent.' Understand that this means the prayer length of a dependent (e.g., a slave) is determined by the master's intention, not the dependent's own.
The original is the one with authority over travel and residence.
2
Define who counts as a dependent (tabi')
The text explicitly mentions three: woman, slave, and soldier. They are dependents because they must obey the original in travel matters. Note that this list is not exhaustive, as later parts extend it.
The author expands on this in later passages.
3
Pinpoint the dispute on knowledge
The text presents two views: one that the dependent becomes bound automatically upon the master's intention (like a legal dismissal), and the preferred view that the dependent must know. Trace the argument for each view, noting the sources cited: Fath al-Qadir and al-Khulasa for the automatic view, and al-Muhit and Sharh al-Tahawi for the knowledge view.
The first opinion (knowledge required) is called 'asahh' (most sound).
4
Evaluate the preferred view's proof
Why is requiring knowledge more correct? Because imposing a ruling before knowledge involves hardship (haraj) and harm (darar), which are legally repelled. Unlike an agent, who can avoid harm by not selling, the dependent is compelled to pray; if harm falls on him due to ignorance, it is from another and must be negated.
This is a classic Hanafi use of istihsan to avoid hardship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلاة باب المسافر كغيره وتعتبر نية الإقامة والسفر من الأصل دون التبع أي المرأة والعبد والجندي.
تذكر أنه صلى الظهر والعصر بغير وضوء قالوا: يجب عليه أن يصلي الظهر ركعتين والعصر أربعاً، ولو صلى الظهر والعصر وهو مقيم ثم سافر قبل غروب الشمس والمسألة بحالها يصلي الظهر أربعاً والعصر ركعتين ا.هـ. قيد بالصلاة لأن المعتبر في الصوم أول جزء من اليوم حتى لو أسلم بعد طلوع الفجر لا يلزمه صوم ذلك اليوم لكونه معياراً.
قوله : (والعاصي (كغيره أي في الترخص برخص المسافر لإطلاق النصوص ولأن السفر الموجب للرخص ليس بمعصية إنما هو فيما جاوره كخروجه عاقاً لوالديه أو عاصياً على الإمام أو أبقاً من مولاه أو خرجت المرأة بلا محرم أو في العدة أو قاطعاً للطريق وقد تكون بعده كما إذا خرج للحج أو للجهاد ثم قطع الطريق. والقبح المجاور لا يعدم المشروعية أصلاً كالصلاة في الأرض المغصوبة والبيع وقت النداء فصلح السفر مناطاً للرخصة قوله : (وتعتبر نية الإقامة والسفر من الأصل دون التبع أي المرأة والعبد والجندي تفسير للتبع لأن الأصل هو المتمكن من الإقامة والسفر دون التبع لكن لا يلزم التبع الإتمام إلا بعد علمه بنية المتبوع كما في توجه الخطاب الشرعي وعزل الوكيل . وقيل : يلزمه كالعزل الحكمي وهو أحوط كما في فتح القدير وهو ظاهر الرواية كما في الخلاصة، والأول أصح لأن في لزوم الحكم قبل العلم حرجاً وضرراً وهو وهو مدفوع شرعاً بخلاف الوكيل فإنه غير ملجأ إلى البيع فإن له أن لا . الضرر بالامتناع عن البيع، فإذا باع بناء على ظاهر أمره ولحقه ضرر كان الضرر : ناشئاً . من وجه ومن جهة الموكل من وجه فيصح العزل حكماً لا قصداً، وههنا التبع مأمور بقصر صلاته منهي عن إتمامها فكان مضطراً ، فلو صار فرضه أربعاً بإقامة الأصل وهو لا يشعر به لحقه ضرر عظيم من جهة غيره بكل وجه وأنه منفي . كذا في المحيط وشرح الطحاوي. وعلى هذا فما في من أن العبد إذا أم مولاه في السفر فنوى المولى الإقامة صحت حتى لو سلم العبد على رأس الركعتين كان عليهما إعادة تلك الصلاة ا هـ . وكذا العبد إذا كان مع مولاه في السفر فباعه من مقيم والعبد كان في الصلاة ينقلب فرضه أربعاً حتى لو سلم على رأس الركعتين كان عليه إعادة تلك الصلاة ا هـ . مبني على غير الصحيح أن فرض عدم علم العبد أو على الكل إن علم أطلق في تبعية المرأة والجندي وقيدوه بأن تستوفي المرأة مهرها المعجل وإلا فلا تكون تبعاً فالعبرة بنيتها لأن لها أن تحبس نفسها عن الزوج للمعجل دون المؤجل ولا تسكن حيث يسكن هو، وبأن يكون الجندي يرزق من بيت المال، فإن كان رزقه في ماله فالعبرة لنيته لأن له أن يذهب حيث شاء لطلب الرزق. وأطلق في العبد فشمل القن والمدبر وأم الولد، وأما المكاتب فينبغي أن لا يكون تبعاً لأن له السفر بغير إذن المولى فلا يلزمه طاعته.
قائده المصنف قصر التبع على هؤلاء الثلاثة بل هو كل من كان تبعاً لإنسان ويلزمه طاعته فيدخل الأجير مع مستأجره، والمحمول مع حامله، والغريم مع صاحب الدين إن كان معسراً مفلساً فإن كان ملياً فالنية إليه لأنه يمكنه قضاء الدين فيقيم في أي موضع شاء. وأما الأعمى مع فإن كان القائد أجيراً فالعبرة لنية الأعمى وإن كان متطوعاً في قيادة تعتبر نيته . والعبد بين شريكين إذا سافر معهما ثم نوى أحدهما الإقامة قيل لا يصير العبد مقيماً لوقوع الشك في صيرورته مقيماً فيبقى مسافراً. وقيل يصير مقيماً ترجيحاً لنية الإقامة احتياطاً لأمر العبادة. كذا في المحيط . ومحله ما إذا لم يكن بينهما مهايأة، فإن كان بينهما مهايأة في الخدمة فإن العبد يصلي صلاة الإقامة، وإذا خدم المولى الذي لم ينو الإقامة يصلي صلاة السفر. وفي نسخة القاضي الإمام : العبد إذا خرج مع مولاه ولا يعلم سير المولى فإنه يسأله إن أخبره أن مسيره مدة السفر صلى صلاة المسافرين، وإن كان دون ذلك صلى صلاة الإقامة، وإن لم يخبره بذلك إن كان مقيماً قيل ذلك صلى صلاة الإقامة، وإن كان مسافراً قبل صلى صلاة المسافرين . كذا في الخلاصة. وفي القنية: مسافر ومقيم اشتريا عبداً الأصح أن العبد يصلي صلاة المقيم. ودخل تحت الجندي الأمير أمير مع الخليفة كما في الخلاصة وفيها وعلى هذا الحجاج إذا وصلوا بغداد شهر رمضان ولم ينو والإقامة صلوا صلاة المقيمين ا هـ . وظاهره أن الحجاج تبع لأمير القافلة وليس كذلك، ولا ينبغي إدخاله في هذا المبحث بل علته أنهم لما علموا أن القافلة لا تخرج إلا بعد خمسة عشر يوماً نزل ذلك منزلة نيتهم الإقامة نصف شهر كما علل به في التجنيس. وفي المحيط: مسلم أسره العدو إن كان مسيرة العدوّ ثلاثة أيام يقصر ، وإن كان دون ذلك يتم، وإن لم يعلم يسأل كما مر في العبد، ولو دخل مسافر مصراً فأخذه غريمه فحبسه، فإن كان معسراً قصر لأنه لم ينو الإقامة ولا يحل للطالب حبسه ، وإن كان موسراً، إن عزم أن يقضي دينه أو لم يعزم شيئاً قصر ، وإن عزم واعتقد أن لا يقضيه أتم والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم بالصواب وإليه المرجع والمآب .
Book of Prayer, Chapter on the Traveler: He is like others, and the intention of residence and travel is considered from the original (master), not from the dependent, i.e., the woman, the slave, and the soldier. It is mentioned that he prayed Zuhr and Asr without ablution; they said: He must pray Zuhr two rak'ahs and Asr four. And if he prayed Zuhr and Asr while resident then traveled before sunset, the case remains: he prays Zuhr four and Asr two. End. It is restricted to prayer because in fasting the first part of the day is considered, such that if one becomes Muslim after dawn, fasting that day is not obligatory because it is a measure. His statement: (And the disobedient is like others), meaning in concession of the traveler's licenses, due to the generality of texts and because travel that necessitates concessions is not disobedience; rather, it is in what accompanies it, such as leaving while being disobedient to parents, or rebellious against the imam, or a runaway from his master, or a woman leaving without a mahram or during 'iddah, or a highway robber. And it may be after that, as when he leaves for Hajj or jihad then robs the road. The accompanying ugliness does not negate the fundamental legality, like prayer in usurped land and sale at the time of the call; so travel is suitable as a reason for concession. His statement: (And the intention of residence and travel is considered from the original, not from the dependent, i.e., the woman, the slave, and the soldier) is an explanation of the dependent, because the original is the one capable of residence and travel, not the dependent. But the dependent is not required to complete (prayer) until after he knows the intention of the principal, as in the direction of legal address and the dismissal of an agent. And it is said: It becomes binding on him like legal dismissal, which is more precautionary, as in Fath al-Qadir, and it is the apparent narration as in al-Khulasa. The first opinion is more correct because requiring the ruling before knowledge involves hardship and harm, and that is repelled legally, unlike the agent, who is not forced to sell; he can avoid harm by refraining from selling. So if he sells based on the apparent matter and harm befalls him, the harm arises from one direction and from the principal's direction in another, so dismissal is valid as a ruling not intentionally. Here, the dependent is commanded to shorten his prayer and prohibited from completing it, so he is compelled. If his obligation becomes four due to the original's residence while he is unaware, great harm from others would befall him in every way, and that is negated. Thus, in al-Muhit and Sharh al-Tahawi. Based on this, what is in... that if a slave leads his master in prayer during travel and the master intends residence, the prayer is valid such that if the slave says salam after two rak'ahs, they must repeat that prayer. And similarly, if a slave is with his master in travel and the master sells him to a resident while the slave is in prayer, his obligation changes to four; so if he says salam after two rak'ahs, he must repeat that prayer. This is based on the incorrect view that the slave is unaware, or otherwise. There is a qualification regarding the dependency of the woman and the soldier: the woman must have received her prompt dowry; otherwise she is not a dependent, so her own intention is considered because she can refuse herself to the husband for the prompt dowry but not the deferred, and she does not reside where he resides. The soldier must receive his salary from the treasury; if his salary is from his own wealth, his own intention is considered because he can go where he wishes seeking provision. Regarding the slave, it is general, including the owned slave, the mudabbar, and the umm walad. As for the mukatab, he should not be a dependent because he can travel without the master's permission, so obedience is not required. The author has restricted the dependent to these three, but rather it is everyone who is dependent on a person and must obey him; thus includes the hired worker with his employer, the carried person with his carrier, and the debtor with the creditor if he is insolvent; if wealthy, the intention is his own because he can repay the debt and thus stay wherever he wishes. As for the blind person with the guide: if the guide is hired, the blind person's intention is considered; if the guide is volunteering, his intention is considered. If a slave is jointly owned by two partners and travels with them, then one intends residence: it is said the slave does not become resident due to doubt, so he remains a traveler; and it is said he becomes resident preferring the intention of residence as a precaution for worship. This applies if there is no division of service; if there is a division, the slave prays the resident's prayer when serving the partner who intended residence, and the traveler's prayer when serving the other. In Qadi al-Imam's copy: if a slave goes out with his master and does not know the master's travel, he asks him. If he informs him that his journey is the duration of travel, he prays the traveler's prayer; if less, he prays the resident's prayer. If he does not inform him, and he was resident at that time, he prays the resident's prayer; if he was traveling before, he prays the traveler's prayer. In al-Qunyah: if a traveler and a resident buy a slave, the correct view is that the slave prays the resident's prayer. The soldier includes the commander with the caliph. And based on this, if the pilgrims arrive in Baghdad during Ramadan and do not intend residence, they pray the resident's prayer. The apparent is that the pilgrims are dependent on the amir of the caravan, but it is not so; it should not be entered into this discussion. Rather, its cause is that when they know the caravan will not leave until after fifteen days, that takes the place of their intention of residence for half a month. In al-Muhit: if a Muslim is captured by the enemy, if the enemy's journey is three days, he shortens; if less, he completes. If he does not know, he asks as with the slave. If a traveler enters a city and his creditor imprisons him, if insolvent he shortens because he did not intend residence; it is not permissible for the claimant to imprison him. If he is wealthy, if he resolves to repay his debt or does not resolve anything, he shortens. If he resolves and believes he will not repay, he completes. And Allah knows best, and to Him is the return and recourse.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the intention of the dependent (tabi') in travel. The main ruling states that for the purpose of shortening prayer (qasr), it is the intention of the original (asl) – the master, husband, or commander – that counts, not the intention of the dependent (woman, slave, soldier). The author then expands on this with several clarifications. First, he discusses the case of a sinful traveler (asi), confirming that even a disobedient traveler is entitled to concessions because the travel itself is not disobedience; the accompanying sin does not negate the legality of the license, analogous to prayer in usurped land being valid (though sinful). Next, he explains the phrase 'from the original, not the dependent' by stating that the dependent does not become bound to complete prayer until they actually know the master's intention. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: some (like the author of Fath al-Qadir) say the ruling becomes binding automatically upon the master's intention, even without the dependent's knowledge, as a 'hukmi' (legal) consequence. However, the preferred opinion is that knowledge is required, to avoid hardship, unlike an agent who can avoid loss by not selling. The text then addresses specific scenarios: a slave leading his master in prayer when the master intends residence – the prayer becomes complete (four rak'ahs), and if the slave says salam after two, he must repeat. Similarly, if a slave is sold to a resident while in prayer, his obligation changes to four. These rulings are based on the assumption of the slave's ignorance; if he knows, the ruling differs. The author also qualifies the dependency of women and soldiers: a woman is not a dependent if she has not received her prompt dowry, because she can refuse to follow her husband; a soldier is not a dependent if he finances his own expenses, as he can choose where to go. Slaves include all types except the mukatab, who can travel without permission. The author then extends the principle of dependency beyond the three mentioned to anyone who must obey another, such as a hired worker, a carried person, an insolvent debtor, and a blind person with a volunteer guide. For a slave jointly owned, if one master intends residence and the other does not, the slave's prayer status follows the master he is currently serving, if there is a division of service. Various other cases are discussed, such as a captured Muslim, a debtor imprisoned by his creditor, and pilgrims staying in Baghdad for more than fifteen days – all with the same underlying principle: the dependent's intention does not matter; the master's intention or the objective duration determines the ruling.

Key Terms
التبع (at-tabi'): The dependent person whose intention in travel is subsumed under that of the original (master, husband, commander).
الأصل (al-asl): The person who has independent authority over their own travel and residence; the master, husband, or commander.
العزل الحكمي (al-'azl al-hukmi): A legal dismissal that takes effect automatically without the dismissed person's knowledge, analogous to the principal's revocation of an agent's authority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi position in this passage, when does a dependent traveler become obligated to complete (pray four rak'ahs) his prayer?
A) Immediately when the master intends residence, even if the dependent does not know.
B) Only when the dependent actually knows of the master's intention of residence.
C) When the dependent himself intends to reside.
D) After the dependent has resided for 15 days.

Key Takeaway
The dependent's prayer length follows the master's intention, but the obligation to complete only arises when the dependent knows of that intention, to avoid hardship.

Extensions and Qualifications of the Dependent Status
Expansion of the dependent concept with specific cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which individuals are considered dependents (tabi') for travel prayer concessions and apply the exceptions and qualifications to scenarios involving women, soldiers, slaves, and others.

Core Idea
Dependency in travel is not absolute: it can be overridden by financial independence or lack of obedience, and it extends beyond the classic three to include all who must obey.

This passage continues the commentary on the dependent's intention, now examining various scenarios and extensions. The author clarifies that the three classic dependents (woman, slave, soldier) have qualifications: a woman is not a dependent if she hasn't received her prompt dowry, a soldier is not if he finances himself. Moreover, the category extends to anyone who must obey another, such as a hired worker, an insolvent debtor, a blind person with a volunteer guide, and others. The lesson works through these extensions and the specific rules for slaves in joint ownership and other situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qualifications for women and soldiers
The text states that a woman is not a dependent if she has not received her prompt dowry, because she can refuse to travel with her husband. Similarly, a soldier paid from his own wealth can choose where to go. This shows that dependency is tied to the inability to resist the master's will.
If the woman has received her dowry, she must obey, so she is a dependent.
2
Note the extensions beyond the three
The author explicitly expands the category to include anyone who is dependent on another and must obey him: the hired worker (with employer), the carried person (with carrier), the insolvent debtor (with creditor), the blind person with a volunteer guide, and even the slave sold to a resident while in prayer. Identify the common thread: all these persons lack the autonomy to choose their travel.
The mukatab slave is excluded because he can travel without permission.
3
Work through the slave scenarios
Several slave cases are presented: (1) A slave leading his master in prayer when the master intends residence – the slave's obligation becomes four rak'ahs immediately (assuming knowledge? the text says based on the incorrect view that the slave is unaware). (2) A slave sold to a resident while in prayer – his prayer changes to four. (3) A slave jointly owned by a traveler and a resident – his prayer status depends on the service division or the master he is currently serving. Note the significance of knowledge and the precautionary approach.
The text mentions two views on the joint slave: one says he remains a traveler due to doubt, another says he becomes resident as a precaution for worship.
4
Apply the principle to new cases
The text gives additional examples: pilgrims arriving in Baghdad for Ramadan; they are not dependents of the amir, but if they know the caravan will stay at least 15 days, they must pray as residents. A captive Muslim follows the enemy's travel distance. A debtor imprisoned by his creditor: if insolvent, he shortens; if wealthy and resolves not to pay, he completes. Practice applying the same principle: the person's ability to determine their own residence.
The underlying rule: the intention of the one who has practical authority (or the objective duration) governs.

قوله : (والعاصي (كغيره أي في الترخص برخص المسافر لإطلاق النصوص ولأن السفر الموجب للرخص ليس بمعصية إنما هو فيما جاوره كخروجه عاقاً لوالديه أو عاصياً على الإمام أو أبقاً من مولاه أو خرجت المرأة بلا محرم أو في العدة أو قاطعاً للطريق وقد تكون بعده كما إذا خرج للحج أو للجهاد ثم قطع الطريق. والقبح المجاور لا يعدم المشروعية أصلاً كالصلاة في الأرض المغصوبة والبيع وقت النداء فصلح السفر مناطاً للرخصة قوله : (وتعتبر نية الإقامة والسفر من الأصل دون التبع أي المرأة والعبد والجندي تفسير للتبع لأن الأصل هو المتمكن من الإقامة والسفر دون التبع لكن لا يلزم التبع الإتمام إلا بعد علمه بنية المتبوع كما في توجه الخطاب الشرعي وعزل الوكيل . وقيل : يلزمه كالعزل الحكمي وهو أحوط كما في فتح القدير وهو ظاهر الرواية كما في الخلاصة، والأول أصح لأن في لزوم الحكم قبل العلم حرجاً وضرراً وهو وهو مدفوع شرعاً بخلاف الوكيل فإنه غير ملجأ إلى البيع فإن له أن لا . الضرر بالامتناع عن البيع، فإذا باع بناء على ظاهر أمره ولحقه ضرر كان الضرر : ناشئاً . من وجه ومن جهة الموكل من وجه فيصح العزل حكماً لا قصداً، وههنا التبع مأمور بقصر صلاته منهي عن إتمامها فكان مضطراً ، فلو صار فرضه أربعاً بإقامة الأصل وهو لا يشعر به لحقه ضرر عظيم من جهة غيره بكل وجه وأنه منفي . كذا في المحيط وشرح الطحاوي. وعلى هذا فما في من أن العبد إذا أم مولاه في السفر فنوى المولى الإقامة صحت حتى لو سلم العبد على رأس الركعتين كان عليهما إعادة تلك الصلاة ا هـ . وكذا العبد إذا كان مع مولاه في السفر فباعه من مقيم والعبد كان في الصلاة ينقلب فرضه أربعاً حتى لو سلم على رأس الركعتين كان عليه إعادة تلك الصلاة ا هـ . مبني على غير الصحيح أن فرض عدم علم العبد أو على الكل إن علم أطلق في تبعية المرأة والجندي وقيدوه بأن تستوفي المرأة مهرها المعجل وإلا فلا تكون تبعاً فالعبرة بنيتها لأن لها أن تحبس نفسها عن الزوج للمعجل دون المؤجل ولا تسكن حيث يسكن هو، وبأن يكون الجندي يرزق من بيت المال، فإن كان رزقه في ماله فالعبرة لنيته لأن له أن يذهب حيث شاء لطلب الرزق. وأطلق في العبد فشمل القن والمدبر وأم الولد، وأما المكاتب فينبغي أن لا يكون تبعاً لأن له السفر بغير إذن المولى فلا يلزمه طاعته.
قائده المصنف قصر التبع على هؤلاء الثلاثة بل هو كل من كان تبعاً لإنسان ويلزمه طاعته فيدخل الأجير مع مستأجره، والمحمول مع حامله، والغريم مع صاحب الدين إن كان معسراً مفلساً فإن كان ملياً فالنية إليه لأنه يمكنه قضاء الدين فيقيم في أي موضع شاء. وأما الأعمى مع فإن كان القائد أجيراً فالعبرة لنية الأعمى وإن كان متطوعاً في قيادة تعتبر نيته . والعبد بين شريكين إذا سافر معهما ثم نوى أحدهما الإقامة قيل لا يصير العبد مقيماً لوقوع الشك في صيرورته مقيماً فيبقى مسافراً. وقيل يصير مقيماً ترجيحاً لنية الإقامة احتياطاً لأمر العبادة. كذا في المحيط . ومحله ما إذا لم يكن بينهما مهايأة، فإن كان بينهما مهايأة في الخدمة فإن العبد يصلي صلاة الإقامة، وإذا خدم المولى الذي لم ينو الإقامة يصلي صلاة السفر. وفي نسخة القاضي الإمام : العبد إذا خرج مع مولاه ولا يعلم سير المولى فإنه يسأله إن أخبره أن مسيره مدة السفر صلى صلاة المسافرين، وإن كان دون ذلك صلى صلاة الإقامة، وإن لم يخبره بذلك إن كان مقيماً قيل ذلك صلى صلاة الإقامة، وإن كان مسافراً قبل صلى صلاة المسافرين . كذا في الخلاصة. وفي القنية: مسافر ومقيم اشتريا عبداً الأصح أن العبد يصلي صلاة المقيم. ودخل تحت الجندي الأمير أمير مع الخليفة كما في الخلاصة وفيها وعلى هذا الحجاج إذا وصلوا بغداد شهر رمضان ولم ينو والإقامة صلوا صلاة المقيمين ا هـ . وظاهره أن الحجاج تبع لأمير القافلة وليس كذلك، ولا ينبغي إدخاله في هذا المبحث بل علته أنهم لما علموا أن القافلة لا تخرج إلا بعد خمسة عشر يوماً نزل ذلك منزلة نيتهم الإقامة نصف شهر كما علل به في التجنيس. وفي المحيط: مسلم أسره العدو إن كان مسيرة العدوّ ثلاثة أيام يقصر ، وإن كان دون ذلك يتم، وإن لم يعلم يسأل كما مر في العبد، ولو دخل مسافر مصراً فأخذه غريمه فحبسه، فإن كان معسراً قصر لأنه لم ينو الإقامة ولا يحل للطالب حبسه ، وإن كان موسراً، إن عزم أن يقضي دينه أو لم يعزم شيئاً قصر ، وإن عزم واعتقد أن لا يقضيه أتم والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم بالصواب وإليه المرجع والمآب .
Book of Prayer, Chapter on the Traveler: He is like others, and the intention of residence and travel is considered from the original (master), not from the dependent, i.e., the woman, the slave, and the soldier. It is mentioned that he prayed Zuhr and Asr without ablution; they said: He must pray Zuhr two rak'ahs and Asr four. And if he prayed Zuhr and Asr while resident then traveled before sunset, the case remains: he prays Zuhr four and Asr two. End. It is restricted to prayer because in fasting the first part of the day is considered, such that if one becomes Muslim after dawn, fasting that day is not obligatory because it is a measure. His statement: (And the disobedient is like others), meaning in concession of the traveler's licenses, due to the generality of texts and because travel that necessitates concessions is not disobedience; rather, it is in what accompanies it, such as leaving while being disobedient to parents, or rebellious against the imam, or a runaway from his master, or a woman leaving without a mahram or during 'iddah, or a highway robber. And it may be after that, as when he leaves for Hajj or jihad then robs the road. The accompanying ugliness does not negate the fundamental legality, like prayer in usurped land and sale at the time of the call; so travel is suitable as a reason for concession. His statement: (And the intention of residence and travel is considered from the original, not from the dependent, i.e., the woman, the slave, and the soldier) is an explanation of the dependent, because the original is the one capable of residence and travel, not the dependent. But the dependent is not required to complete (prayer) until after he knows the intention of the principal, as in the direction of legal address and the dismissal of an agent. And it is said: It becomes binding on him like legal dismissal, which is more precautionary, as in Fath al-Qadir, and it is the apparent narration as in al-Khulasa. The first opinion is more correct because requiring the ruling before knowledge involves hardship and harm, and that is repelled legally, unlike the agent, who is not forced to sell; he can avoid harm by refraining from selling. So if he sells based on the apparent matter and harm befalls him, the harm arises from one direction and from the principal's direction in another, so dismissal is valid as a ruling not intentionally. Here, the dependent is commanded to shorten his prayer and prohibited from completing it, so he is compelled. If his obligation becomes four due to the original's residence while he is unaware, great harm from others would befall him in every way, and that is negated. Thus, in al-Muhit and Sharh al-Tahawi. Based on this, what is in... that if a slave leads his master in prayer during travel and the master intends residence, the prayer is valid such that if the slave says salam after two rak'ahs, they must repeat that prayer. And similarly, if a slave is with his master in travel and the master sells him to a resident while the slave is in prayer, his obligation changes to four; so if he says salam after two rak'ahs, he must repeat that prayer. This is based on the incorrect view that the slave is unaware, or otherwise. There is a qualification regarding the dependency of the woman and the soldier: the woman must have received her prompt dowry; otherwise she is not a dependent, so her own intention is considered because she can refuse herself to the husband for the prompt dowry but not the deferred, and she does not reside where he resides. The soldier must receive his salary from the treasury; if his salary is from his own wealth, his own intention is considered because he can go where he wishes seeking provision. Regarding the slave, it is general, including the owned slave, the mudabbar, and the umm walad. As for the mukatab, he should not be a dependent because he can travel without the master's permission, so obedience is not required. The author has restricted the dependent to these three, but rather it is everyone who is dependent on a person and must obey him; thus includes the hired worker with his employer, the carried person with his carrier, and the debtor with the creditor if he is insolvent; if wealthy, the intention is his own because he can repay the debt and thus stay wherever he wishes. As for the blind person with the guide: if the guide is hired, the blind person's intention is considered; if the guide is volunteering, his intention is considered. If a slave is jointly owned by two partners and travels with them, then one intends residence: it is said the slave does not become resident due to doubt, so he remains a traveler; and it is said he becomes resident preferring the intention of residence as a precaution for worship. This applies if there is no division of service; if there is a division, the slave prays the resident's prayer when serving the partner who intended residence, and the traveler's prayer when serving the other. In Qadi al-Imam's copy: if a slave goes out with his master and does not know the master's travel, he asks him. If he informs him that his journey is the duration of travel, he prays the traveler's prayer; if less, he prays the resident's prayer. If he does not inform him, and he was resident at that time, he prays the resident's prayer; if he was traveling before, he prays the traveler's prayer. In al-Qunyah: if a traveler and a resident buy a slave, the correct view is that the slave prays the resident's prayer. The soldier includes the commander with the caliph. And based on this, if the pilgrims arrive in Baghdad during Ramadan and do not intend residence, they pray the resident's prayer. The apparent is that the pilgrims are dependent on the amir of the caravan, but it is not so; it should not be entered into this discussion. Rather, its cause is that when they know the caravan will not leave until after fifteen days, that takes the place of their intention of residence for half a month. In al-Muhit: if a Muslim is captured by the enemy, if the enemy's journey is three days, he shortens; if less, he completes. If he does not know, he asks as with the slave. If a traveler enters a city and his creditor imprisons him, if insolvent he shortens because he did not intend residence; it is not permissible for the claimant to imprison him. If he is wealthy, if he resolves to repay his debt or does not resolve anything, he shortens. If he resolves and believes he will not repay, he completes. And Allah knows best, and to Him is the return and recourse.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the intention of the dependent (tabi') in travel. The main ruling states that for the purpose of shortening prayer (qasr), it is the intention of the original (asl) – the master, husband, or commander – that counts, not the intention of the dependent (woman, slave, soldier). The author then expands on this with several clarifications. First, he discusses the case of a sinful traveler (asi), confirming that even a disobedient traveler is entitled to concessions because the travel itself is not disobedience; the accompanying sin does not negate the legality of the license, analogous to prayer in usurped land being valid (though sinful). Next, he explains the phrase 'from the original, not the dependent' by stating that the dependent does not become bound to complete prayer until they actually know the master's intention. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: some (like the author of Fath al-Qadir) say the ruling becomes binding automatically upon the master's intention, even without the dependent's knowledge, as a 'hukmi' (legal) consequence. However, the preferred opinion is that knowledge is required, to avoid hardship, unlike an agent who can avoid loss by not selling. The text then addresses specific scenarios: a slave leading his master in prayer when the master intends residence – the prayer becomes complete (four rak'ahs), and if the slave says salam after two, he must repeat. Similarly, if a slave is sold to a resident while in prayer, his obligation changes to four. These rulings are based on the assumption of the slave's ignorance; if he knows, the ruling differs. The author also qualifies the dependency of women and soldiers: a woman is not a dependent if she has not received her prompt dowry, because she can refuse to follow her husband; a soldier is not a dependent if he finances his own expenses, as he can choose where to go. Slaves include all types except the mukatab, who can travel without permission. The author then extends the principle of dependency beyond the three mentioned to anyone who must obey another, such as a hired worker, a carried person, an insolvent debtor, and a blind person with a volunteer guide. For a slave jointly owned, if one master intends residence and the other does not, the slave's prayer status follows the master he is currently serving, if there is a division of service. Various other cases are discussed, such as a captured Muslim, a debtor imprisoned by his creditor, and pilgrims staying in Baghdad for more than fifteen days – all with the same underlying principle: the dependent's intention does not matter; the master's intention or the objective duration determines the ruling.

Key Terms
التبع (at-tabi'): The dependent person whose intention in travel is subsumed under that of the original (master, husband, commander).
المهر المعجل (al-mahr al-mu'ajjal): The prompt dowry given to the wife; if not received, she may refuse to travel with her husband.
المهابأة (al-muhaya'ah): A division of service between co-owners of a slave, where the slave serves each owner on different days or in different capacities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition is a female traveler NOT considered a dependent (tabi') such that her own intention determines her prayer length?
A) If she has not received her prompt dowry.
B) If she is traveling without a mahram.
C) If she is on a pilgrimage.
D) If she has children.

Key Takeaway
Dependency in travel is determined by the obligation to obey; financial independence or legal autonomy can remove a person from this category.

Salah — Prayer

Obligation and Conditions of Friday Prayer
Definition of obligation and conditions of Jumu‘ah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that Friday prayer is a distinct fard 'ayn, list its six conditions of validity, and explain why denying its obligation constitutes disbelief.

Core Idea
Friday prayer is an independent obligation, not a reduced Zuhr; denying it is apostasy.

Friday prayer (Salat al-Jumu‘ah) holds a unique status in the Hanafi school. It is not merely a substitute for Zuhr but a separate fard established by definitive proof. This lesson clarifies the true Hanafi position, corrects a common misconception, and outlines the essential conditions for its validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Determine the level of obligation for Jumu‘ah: it is fard ‘ayn, more emphatic than Zuhr. Understand that denial of its obligation is kufr (disbelief).
This is established by Quran, Sunnah, and consensus.
2
Refute the misconception
Explain why Jumu‘ah is not a split of Zuhr and why the practice of praying 4 rak‘ahs after it with Zuhr intention is baseless and not from the Hanafi founders.
The commentator strongly rejects this practice.
3
List the conditions of validity
Memorize the six conditions for Jumu‘ah to be valid: misr (city), sultan (ruler), time (after zawal), khutbah (sermon), jama‘ah (congregation), and adhan ‘amm (general call).
These are conditions of validity, not obligation.
4
Distinguish conditions of obligation
Note that there are also six conditions for obligation (to be covered later). They are separate from conditions of validity.
The passage only lists validity conditions; obligation conditions are mentioned but not detailed.

كتاب الصلاة باب صلاة الجمعة
شرط أدائها المصر وهو كل موضع له أمير وقاض ينفذ الأحكام ويقيم الحدود أو تعددها
تنصيف الظهر بعينه بل هي فرض ابتداء نسبته النصف منها وهي فريضة محكمة بالكتاب والسنة والإجماع يكفر جاحدها. وقد أطال المحقق في فتح القدير في بيان دلائلها ثم قال : وإنما أكثرنا فيه نوعاً من الإكثار لما نسمع عن بعض الجهلة أنهم ينسبون إلى مذهب الحنفية عدم افتراضها، ومنشأ غلطهم ما سيأتي من قول القدوري : ومن صلى الظهر في منزله يوم الجمعة ولا عذر له كره وجازت صلاته وإنما أراد حرم عليه وصحت الظهر فالحرمة لترك الفرض وصحه الظهر لما سنذكره، وقد صرح أصحابنا بأنها فرض آكد من الظهر وبإكفار جاحدها ا هـ. أقول : وقد كثر ذلك من جهلة زماننا أيضاً ومنشأ جهلهم صلاة الأربع بعد الجمعة بنية الظهر، وإنما وضعها بعض المتأخرين عند الشك في صحة الجمعة بسبب رواية في مصر واحد وليست هذه الرواية بالمختارة، وليس هذا القول ـ أعني اختيار صلاة الأربع بعدها - مروياً عن أبي حنيفة وصاحبيه حتى وقع لي أني أفتيت مراراً بعدم صلاتها خوفاً على اعتقاد الجهلة بأنها الفرض وأن الجمعة ليست بفرض، وسنوضحه من بعد إن شاء الله تعالى. وأما شرائطها فنوعان شرائط صحة وشرائط وجوب؛ فالأول ستة كما ذكره المصنف : المصر والسلطان والوقت والخطبة والجماعة والأذان العام. والثاني ستة أيضاً كما سيأتي وهي بضم الميم وإسكانها وفتحها حكى ذلك الفراء والواحدي من الاجتماع كالفرقة من الافتراق أضيف إليها اليوم والصلاة ثم كثر الاستعمال حتى حذف منها المضاف وجمعت فقيل جمعات كذا وجمع . في المغرب. وكان. يوم الجمعة في الجاهلية يسمى عروبة بفتح العين المهملة وضم الراء وبالباء الموحدة، وأول من سماها يوم الجمعة كعب بن لؤي، ولما قدم رسول الله له المدينة أقام يوم الاثنين والثلاثاء والأربعاء والخميس في بني عمرو بن عوف وأسس مسجدهم ثم خرج من عندهم فأدركته الجمعة في بني سالم بن عوف فصلاها في المسجد الذي في بطن الوادي - وادي راتونا - فكانت أوّل جمعة صلاها عليه الصلاة والسلام بالمدينة .
:
قوله (شرط أدائها المصر) أي شرط صحتها أن تؤدى في مصر حتى لا تصح في قرية ولا مفازة لقول علي رضي الله عنه : لا جمعة ولا تشريق ولا صلاة فطر ولا أضحى إلا في مصر جامع أو في مدينة عظيمة. رواه ابن أبي شيبة وصححه ابن حزم، وكفى بقوله قدوة وإماماً. وإذا لم تصح في غير المصر فلا تجب على غير أهله. وفي الخلاصة: القروي إذا دخل يوم الجمعة إن نوى أن يمكث فيه يوم الجمعة لزمته ،الجمعة وإن نوى الخروج من ذلك المصر من يومه قبل دخول وقت الصلاة لا تلزمه وبعد دخول وقت الجمعة تلزمه. قال
المصر
Chapter of Friday Prayer: Prayer. What preceded it is the splitting of prayer due to an excuse, but the splitting here is specific in its appropriateness with Zuhr, and what preceded it was in every four-rak‘ah prayer, and presenting the general is the proper way. And we do not mean that Friday is a splitting of Zuhr itself, rather it is an initial obligation, its proportion is half of it, and it is a firmly established obligation by the Book, the Sunnah, and consensus; its denier becomes a disbeliever. The investigator in Fath al-Qadir expanded in explaining its proofs, then said: We have expanded somewhat because we hear from some ignorant people that they attribute to the Hanafi school the non-obligation of it. The source of their error is what will come from the statement of al-Quduri: 'Whoever prays Zuhr in his home on Friday without an excuse, it is disliked and his prayer is valid.' He meant it is prohibited and his Zuhr is valid, the prohibition is for leaving the obligation and the validity of Zuhr is as we will mention. Our companions have clearly stated that it is a more emphatic obligation than Zuhr and that its denier becomes a disbeliever. I say: This has become frequent from the ignorant of our time too. The source of their ignorance is the prayer of four rak‘ahs after Friday with the intention of Zuhr. This was instituted by some later scholars due to doubt about the validity of Friday because of a narration about a single city, and this narration is not chosen. And this statement—meaning the choice of praying four after it—is not narrated from Abu Hanifa and his two companions, such that I have given fatwa repeatedly to not pray it, fearing that the ignorant believe that it is the obligation and that Friday is not an obligation. We will clarify later, God willing. As for its conditions, they are two types: conditions of validity and conditions of obligation. The first are six as the author mentioned: the city (misr), the ruler (sultan), the time, the sermon (khutbah), the congregation (jama‘ah), and the general call (adhan ‘amm). The second are also six as will come. And the word Jumu‘ah is with damma, sukun, and fatha on the meem, as narrated by al-Farra’ and al-Wahidi. It is derived from social gathering like farqah from parting. The day and prayer were added, then usage increased until the added word was dropped and it was pluralized as jumu‘at. In al-Mughrib. The day of Jumu‘ah in the Jahiliyyah was called ‘Arubah (with fatha and ra and ba). The first to name it Jumu‘ah was Ka‘b ibn Lu’ayy. When the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) arrived in Medina, he stayed Monday, Tuesday, Wednesday, and Thursday with Banu ‘Amr ibn ‘Awf and founded their mosque. Then he left them and Friday caught up with him at Banu Salim ibn ‘Awf, and he prayed it in the mosque in the valley—Wadi Ratuna—and that was the first Friday he prayed in Madinah. His statement: 'Condition of performing it is the city (misr)' means a condition of its validity is that it be performed in a city, so it is not valid in a village or desert, due to the statement of ‘Ali (may God be pleased with him): 'There is no Friday, no tashriq, no ‘Eid prayer except in a large city (misr jami‘) or a great town.' Narrated by Ibn Abi Shaybah and authenticated by Ibn Hazm. Sufficient as a leader and imam. And if it is not valid outside the city, it is not obligatory on its non-residents. In al-Khulasa: A villager who enters on Friday: if he intends to stay on Friday, Friday becomes obligatory on him; if he intends to leave that city on the same day before the time of prayer enters, it does not become obligatory; and after the time of Friday enters, it becomes obligatory. He said: the city.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on Friday Prayer (Bab Salat al-Jumu‘ah). It begins by clarifying a misconception: Friday prayer is not a shortened version of Zuhr (as some erroneously claim) but is a distinct primary obligation (fard) established by the Quran, Sunnah, and consensus. The text emphasizes that denying its obligation leads to apostasy (kufr). The commentator quotes from Fath al-Qadir detailing the evidence, then addresses a misunderstanding arising from al-Quduri's statement about praying Zuhr at home on Friday, which is actually prohibited but valid if done. The commentator attributes the confusion to later scholars recommending a four-rak‘ah prayer after Friday with the intention of Zuhr due to doubts about the validity of Friday in some cities. He strongly refutes this practice, stating it is not from the Hanafi founders, and that he himself has given fatwa against it. Then the passage lists the six conditions for validity of Friday prayer: the city (misr), the ruler (sultan), the time (after zawal), the sermon (khutbah), the congregation (jama‘ah), and the general call to prayer (adhan ‘amm). It also mentions six conditions for obligation, to be discussed later. The etymology of Jumu‘ah and its historical background are provided, including that it was called 'Arubah in pre-Islamic times and the first to name it Jumu‘ah was Ka‘b ibn Lu’ayy. The first Friday prayer of the Prophet in Medina is described. Finally, the text elaborates on the condition of 'misr' (city), quoting the hadith of ‘Ali, and explains that a villager entering the city on Friday becomes obliged to pray Jumu‘ah if he intends to stay, with details based on time of entry.

Key Terms
جمعة (Jumu‘ah): The Friday congregational prayer, a distinct fard replacing Zuhr.
مصر (misr): A large city with a ruler and judge who execute laws and punishments; a condition for validity of Jumu‘ah.
خطبة (khutbah): The sermon delivered before Jumu‘ah prayer; a condition of validity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many conditions of validity for Friday prayer are listed in the passage?
A) 4
B) 5
C) 6
D) 7

Key Takeaway
Friday prayer is a distinct fard 'ayn, with six conditions for validity; it is not a replacement for Zuhr, and its denial is apostasy.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate
8 min
Friday Prayer is an Independent Fard with the Condition of ‘Misr’
Ruling with proof: Friday obligation and condition of ‘مصر’

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that Friday prayer is an independent fard and explain the condition of ‘مصر’ for its validity.

Core Idea
Friday prayer is a distinct fard, not half of zuhr; its validity requires a city with an amir and qadi.

This lesson establishes the fundamental Hanafi position that Friday prayer is an independent religious obligation. We then examine the first condition for its validity: that it be performed in a ‘مصر’ (metropolis). The proof from the hadith of Ali (RA) and its implications are explained.

Guided Path
1
Identify the obligatory status of Friday prayer
Note that the text declares Friday prayer as a firmly established fard (فريضة محكمة) and that denying it is kufr.
This refutes the misconception that Hanafis consider it less than obligatory.
2
Recognise the condition of ‘مصر’
The text states that the condition for its performance is the metropolis (شرط أدائها المصر). Friday prayer is not valid in a village or desert.
This is a shart of validity (شرط صحة), not just obligation.
3
Examine the dalil
The hadith of Ali (RA) is cited: 'No Friday, no tashriq, no Eid prayer, no Adha except in a congregational metropolis or a great city.'
The phrase 'لا...إلا' indicates exclusivity, making the condition necessary.
4
Note the consequence for non-residents
If Friday is not valid outside the metropolis, then it is not obligatory on those outside it. Special cases for travelers and villagers are given in the text.
Refer to the rulings from al-Khulasa about villagers entering the city.

Book of Prayer, Chapter of Friday Prayer. The condition for its performance is the metropolis, and it is every place that has an amir and a qadi who execute judgments and enforce the hudud, or its multiplicity. It is not half of zuhr itself; rather it is an independent obligation whose proportion is half of it, and it is a firmly established obligation by the Book, Sunnah, and consensus; its denier becomes a disbeliever. The investigator in Fath al-Qadir extensively discussed its proofs, then said: We have elaborated somewhat because we hear from some ignorant people that they attribute to the Hanafi school that it is not obligatory. The source of their error is what will come from al-Quduri: 'Whoever prays zuhr in his house on Friday without an excuse, it is disliked but his prayer is valid.' He meant that it is forbidden upon him but his zuhr is valid; the prohibition is for leaving the obligation, and the validity of zuhr is for what we will mention. Our companions have explicitly stated that it is more emphatically obligatory than zuhr and that its denier becomes a disbeliever. I say: This has become common among the ignorant of our time too, and the source of their ignorance is praying four rak'ahs after Friday with the intention of zuhr. Some later scholars introduced this due to doubt about the validity of Friday because of a narration about a single metropolis, but this narration is not the chosen one. This statement—choosing to pray four after it—is not transmitted from Abu Hanifa and his two companions, so much so that I have given fatwa repeatedly not to pray it, fearing that the ignorant would believe it to be the obligation and that Friday is not obligatory. We will clarify later, God willing. As for its conditions, they are of two types: conditions of validity and conditions of obligation. The first are six as mentioned by the author: the metropolis, the sultan, time, the sermon, the congregation, and the general call to prayer. The second are also six as will come. It is with damma, sukun, and fatha of the mim; al-Farra' and al-Wahidi narrated that. From the root 'social gathering' like 'division' from 'separation'; the day and prayer were added to it, then usage became frequent until the annexed part was omitted, and it was pluralized as 'jumu'at'; similarly in al-Mughrib. Friday in the pre-Islamic era was called 'Uruuqa' with a fatha on the 'ayn, damma on the ra, and with a ba. The first to call it 'Friday' was Ka'b ibn Lu'ayy. When the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) arrived in Medina, he stayed Monday, Tuesday, Wednesday, and Thursday with Banu 'Amr ibn 'Awf and founded their mosque. Then he left them, and Friday overtook him at Banu Salim ibn 'Awf, so he prayed it in the mosque in the bottom of the valley—Wadi Ratuna—and that was the first Friday he prayed in Medina. His statement: 'The condition for its performance is the metropolis' means the condition for its validity is that it be performed in a metropolis, so it is not valid in a village or desert, due to the saying of Ali (may God be pleased with him): 'No Friday, no tashriq, no 'Eid prayer, no 'Adha except in a congregational metropolis or a great city.' Narrated by Ibn Abi Shayba and authenticated by Ibn Hazm. Sufficient is his saying as a leader and imam. If it is not valid outside the metropolis, it is not obligatory upon those outside it. In al-Khulasa: If a villager enters on Friday intending to stay that Friday, it becomes obligatory upon him. If he intends to leave that metropolis on the same day before the entry of prayer time, it is not obligatory; after the entry of Friday time, it becomes obligatory. The faqih said: If he intends to leave that same day, even if it is after the entry of Friday time, it is not obligatory. For a city dweller who wants to travel on Friday, there is no harm if he leaves the built-up area before the exit of zuhr time, because Friday becomes obligatory only at the end of the time, and he is a traveler at the end of the time. A traveler who arrives at the metropolis on Friday with the intention of not leaving that Friday does not have Friday obligatory upon him unless he intends to stay fifteen days. End. His statement: 'It is every place that has an amir and a qadi who execute judgments and enforce the hudud' means the definition of the mentioned metropolis is apparent in the school, as stated by Imam al-Sarakhsi. In al-Khulasa, he added: and the mufti is required if the qadi or ruler is not a mufti. In al-Zahiriyya, the amir is omitted, and it says: 'The metropolis in the apparent narration is that it contains a mufti and a qadi who enforces the hudud and executes judgments, and its buildings reach the size of Mina's buildings.' End. The author specified 'enforces the hudud' to exclude the arbitrator and a woman if she is a qadi, because they do not enforce hudud even if they execute judgments. He sufficed with hudud over qisas because whoever has the power to enforce them has the power over qisas. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. Apparently, if a town's qadi or amir is a woman, it is not a metropolis, so Friday cannot be established there; but the apparent is otherwise. He said in al-Bada'i: As for a woman and a discerning child, they cannot establish Friday because they are not suitable for leading prayers in general, so in Friday it is more so. However, if a woman is a sultan and orders a man who is suitable to be imam to lead them in Friday prayer, it is permissible, because a woman is eligible as a sultan or judge in a general sense, so her delegation is valid. End. There are many opinions on the definition of metropolis, from which they chose two: one from al-Mukhtasar, and the second attributed to Abu Hanifa: it is a large town with streets, markets, and rural districts, with a ruler capable of giving justice to the oppressed from the oppressor with dignity and his knowledge or the knowledge of others, and people refer to him for events. He said in al-Bada'i: This is the most correct, and the commentator followed him, and it is the most specific from al-Mukhtasar. And regarding: 'before the exit of zuhr time' in some copies occurs 'before entry' instead of 'exit', which matches what is in al-Zahiriyya, but in al-Khulasa it is 'exit'. The author will address this issue later. His statement: 'Ibn al-Muqaffa' by his saying 'enforces the hudud' etc.' This is according to what several commentators of al-Hidayah have chosen regarding the conjunction of different things; otherwise it is said to be the conjunction of the specific to the general, emphasizing them due to their increased gravity. The first view was objected to in al-Hawashi al-Sa'diyya because the lam-alif in 'ahkam' if for generality, which is apparent since there is no covenant, nullifies what he said. He said in al-Nahr: I say, why can it not be for the genus? Rather, applying it here is more appropriate, since the principle in conjunction is differentiation, and the principle in lam-alif when there is no specified covenant is generality according to the majority, although the mental covenant is advanced according to Sadr al-Shari'a; this is in conflict with the mentioned principle. His statement: 'and the apparent is otherwise' etc. He said in al-Nahr: There is some consideration; perhaps its face is that what is in al-Bada'i might be regarding when there is in her town an amir and qadi who execute judgments and enforce hudud, so it is not text in the claimed matter, so let it be considered. Said by Shaykh Isma'il. And he said in al-Shurnublaliyya: And what the author of al-Bahr said requires consideration because the discussion is about the deputy of the sultan when...
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage from 'Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq' is a commentary on the conditions for Friday prayer. It begins by establishing that Friday prayer is an independent obligatory prayer (fard) distinct from zuhr, and its denier becomes a disbeliever. The author then clarifies a common misconception that the Hanafi school does not consider it obligatory, which arises from the practice of praying four rak'ahs after Jumu'ah out of doubt. He emphasizes that this practice is not from the school's early authorities.

The main focus is the condition of 'مصر' (metropolis) for the validity of Friday prayer. The author states that the Friday prayer must be performed in a city that has an amir (ruler) and a qadi (judge) who execute judicial decisions and enforce the hudud (fixed penal laws). This is proven by the hadith of Ali (RA) reported by Ibn Abi Shaybah: 'No Friday, no tashriq, no Eid prayer, no Adha except in a congregational metropolis or a great city.'

The passage then elaborates on the definition of 'مصر'. The relied-upon view (zahir al-riwayah) as per Imam al-Sarakhsi is that it must have an amir and a qadi. Some authorities also require a mufti. The definition also includes that the city's buildings be as large as those of Mina. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: some omit the requirement of an amir, requiring only a mufti and a qadi. The author presents two main definitions: one from al-Mukhtasar and another attributed to Abu Hanifa himself, describing a large town with streets, markets, rural districts, and a powerful ruler capable of delivering justice. Al-Bada'i considers this latter definition the most correct.

Special cases are discussed: a villager entering the city on Friday becomes obligated if he intends to stay; a traveler entering without intending to stay is not obligated unless he intends to reside for fifteen days. The passage also addresses the case of a female qadi or ruler. The apparent ruling from Fath al-Qadir is that a town with a female qadi is not a valid metropolis, but the author opines otherwise, citing al-Bada'i that a woman can act as sultan and delegate a male imam for Friday prayer.

Finally, there is a grammatical discussion about the conjunction in the phrase 'enforcing the hudud'—whether it is a conjunction of distinct things or specific to general. The author sides with the latter view for emphasis.

Overall, this passage establishes that Friday prayer requires an urban settlement with executive and judicial authority.

Key Terms
مصر (Misr): A city with an amir and qadi who have the authority to enforce hudud and execute judgments, and with a minimum size of buildings like Mina.
أمير (Amir): A ruler or governor with executive power to enforce law and order.
قاض (Qadi): A judge who has authority to adjudicate disputes and enforce Islamic punishments.
حدود (Hudud): Fixed penal laws prescribed by Shari'ah, such as for theft and adultery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the primary condition for Friday prayer to be valid regarding the location?
A) It must be performed in a large city with a population of at least 40.
B) It must be performed in a city that has an amir and qadi who enforce hudud and judgments.
C) It can be performed in any village or desert as long as there is a mosque.
D) It must be performed in a city with a university and hospitals.

Key Takeaway
Friday prayer is an independent fard, not a substitute for zuhr, and its validity requires a city with executive and judicial authority.

Salah — Prayer
advanced
10 min
Detailed Definition of ‘Misr’ and Intra-Hanafi Differences
Expansion probe on definition of 'مصر'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the various Hanafi definitions of ‘مصر’ and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
The relied-upon definition of ‘مصر’ requires a ruler with power to enforce justice, though some authorities omit the amir.

This lesson delves into the detailed definition of ‘مصر’ as a condition for Friday prayer, focusing on the intra-Hanafi differences. We explore the views of al-Sarakhsi, al-Khulasa, al-Zahiriyya, and Abu Hanifa himself, and learn which position is most correct according to later authorities.

Guided Path
1
List the elements of Zahir al-Riwayah definition
Imam al-Sarakhsi states that the metropolis must have an amir and a qadi who execute judgments and enforce hudud.
This is the starting point; subsequent authors add or omit elements.
2
Note the addition of a mufti in al-Khulasa
Al-Khulasa adds the condition of a mufti if the qadi or ruler is not a mufti.
This ensures religious authority is present.
3
Identify the omission of the amir in al-Zahiriyya
In al-Zahiriyya, the amir is dropped; only a mufti and qadi are required, plus buildings as large as Mina's.
This reflects a more flexible view.
4
Study Abu Hanifa's own definition and its acceptance
Abu Hanifa defined it as a large town with streets, markets, rural districts, and a powerful ruler capable of justice. Al-Bada'i declares this the most correct, and the commentator follows it.
This definition is considered the most specific and comprehensive.

Book of Prayer, Chapter of Friday Prayer. The condition for its performance is the metropolis, and it is every place that has an amir and a qadi who execute judgments and enforce the hudud, or its multiplicity. It is not half of zuhr itself; rather it is an independent obligation whose proportion is half of it, and it is a firmly established obligation by the Book, Sunnah, and consensus; its denier becomes a disbeliever. The investigator in Fath al-Qadir extensively discussed its proofs, then said: We have elaborated somewhat because we hear from some ignorant people that they attribute to the Hanafi school that it is not obligatory. The source of their error is what will come from al-Quduri: 'Whoever prays zuhr in his house on Friday without an excuse, it is disliked but his prayer is valid.' He meant that it is forbidden upon him but his zuhr is valid; the prohibition is for leaving the obligation, and the validity of zuhr is for what we will mention. Our companions have explicitly stated that it is more emphatically obligatory than zuhr and that its denier becomes a disbeliever. I say: This has become common among the ignorant of our time too, and the source of their ignorance is praying four rak'ahs after Friday with the intention of zuhr. Some later scholars introduced this due to doubt about the validity of Friday because of a narration about a single metropolis, but this narration is not the chosen one. This statement—choosing to pray four after it—is not transmitted from Abu Hanifa and his two companions, so much so that I have given fatwa repeatedly not to pray it, fearing that the ignorant would believe it to be the obligation and that Friday is not obligatory. We will clarify later, God willing. As for its conditions, they are of two types: conditions of validity and conditions of obligation. The first are six as mentioned by the author: the metropolis, the sultan, time, the sermon, the congregation, and the general call to prayer. The second are also six as will come. It is with damma, sukun, and fatha of the mim; al-Farra' and al-Wahidi narrated that. From the root 'social gathering' like 'division' from 'separation'; the day and prayer were added to it, then usage became frequent until the annexed part was omitted, and it was pluralized as 'jumu'at'; similarly in al-Mughrib. Friday in the pre-Islamic era was called 'Uruuqa' with a fatha on the 'ayn, damma on the ra, and with a ba. The first to call it 'Friday' was Ka'b ibn Lu'ayy. When the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) arrived in Medina, he stayed Monday, Tuesday, Wednesday, and Thursday with Banu 'Amr ibn 'Awf and founded their mosque. Then he left them, and Friday overtook him at Banu Salim ibn 'Awf, so he prayed it in the mosque in the bottom of the valley—Wadi Ratuna—and that was the first Friday he prayed in Medina. His statement: 'The condition for its performance is the metropolis' means the condition for its validity is that it be performed in a metropolis, so it is not valid in a village or desert, due to the saying of Ali (may God be pleased with him): 'No Friday, no tashriq, no 'Eid prayer, no 'Adha except in a congregational metropolis or a great city.' Narrated by Ibn Abi Shayba and authenticated by Ibn Hazm. Sufficient is his saying as a leader and imam. If it is not valid outside the metropolis, it is not obligatory upon those outside it. In al-Khulasa: If a villager enters on Friday intending to stay that Friday, it becomes obligatory upon him. If he intends to leave that metropolis on the same day before the entry of prayer time, it is not obligatory; after the entry of Friday time, it becomes obligatory. The faqih said: If he intends to leave that same day, even if it is after the entry of Friday time, it is not obligatory. For a city dweller who wants to travel on Friday, there is no harm if he leaves the built-up area before the exit of zuhr time, because Friday becomes obligatory only at the end of the time, and he is a traveler at the end of the time. A traveler who arrives at the metropolis on Friday with the intention of not leaving that Friday does not have Friday obligatory upon him unless he intends to stay fifteen days. End. His statement: 'It is every place that has an amir and a qadi who execute judgments and enforce the hudud' means the definition of the mentioned metropolis is apparent in the school, as stated by Imam al-Sarakhsi. In al-Khulasa, he added: and the mufti is required if the qadi or ruler is not a mufti. In al-Zahiriyya, the amir is omitted, and it says: 'The metropolis in the apparent narration is that it contains a mufti and a qadi who enforces the hudud and executes judgments, and its buildings reach the size of Mina's buildings.' End. The author specified 'enforces the hudud' to exclude the arbitrator and a woman if she is a qadi, because they do not enforce hudud even if they execute judgments. He sufficed with hudud over qisas because whoever has the power to enforce them has the power over qisas. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. Apparently, if a town's qadi or amir is a woman, it is not a metropolis, so Friday cannot be established there; but the apparent is otherwise. He said in al-Bada'i: As for a woman and a discerning child, they cannot establish Friday because they are not suitable for leading prayers in general, so in Friday it is more so. However, if a woman is a sultan and orders a man who is suitable to be imam to lead them in Friday prayer, it is permissible, because a woman is eligible as a sultan or judge in a general sense, so her delegation is valid. End. There are many opinions on the definition of metropolis, from which they chose two: one from al-Mukhtasar, and the second attributed to Abu Hanifa: it is a large town with streets, markets, and rural districts, with a ruler capable of giving justice to the oppressed from the oppressor with dignity and his knowledge or the knowledge of others, and people refer to him for events. He said in al-Bada'i: This is the most correct, and the commentator followed him, and it is the most specific from al-Mukhtasar. And regarding: 'before the exit of zuhr time' in some copies occurs 'before entry' instead of 'exit', which matches what is in al-Zahiriyya, but in al-Khulasa it is 'exit'. The author will address this issue later. His statement: 'Ibn al-Muqaffa' by his saying 'enforces the hudud' etc.' This is according to what several commentators of al-Hidayah have chosen regarding the conjunction of different things; otherwise it is said to be the conjunction of the specific to the general, emphasizing them due to their increased gravity. The first view was objected to in al-Hawashi al-Sa'diyya because the lam-alif in 'ahkam' if for generality, which is apparent since there is no covenant, nullifies what he said. He said in al-Nahr: I say, why can it not be for the genus? Rather, applying it here is more appropriate, since the principle in conjunction is differentiation, and the principle in lam-alif when there is no specified covenant is generality according to the majority, although the mental covenant is advanced according to Sadr al-Shari'a; this is in conflict with the mentioned principle. His statement: 'and the apparent is otherwise' etc. He said in al-Nahr: There is some consideration; perhaps its face is that what is in al-Bada'i might be regarding when there is in her town an amir and qadi who execute judgments and enforce hudud, so it is not text in the claimed matter, so let it be considered. Said by Shaykh Isma'il. And he said in al-Shurnublaliyya: And what the author of al-Bahr said requires consideration because the discussion is about the deputy of the sultan when...

Commentary
This passage from 'Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq' is a commentary on the conditions for Friday prayer. It begins by establishing that Friday prayer is an independent obligatory prayer (fard) distinct from zuhr, and its denier becomes a disbeliever. The author then clarifies a common misconception that the Hanafi school does not consider it obligatory, which arises from the practice of praying four rak'ahs after Jumu'ah out of doubt. He emphasizes that this practice is not from the school's early authorities.

The main focus is the condition of 'مصر' (metropolis) for the validity of Friday prayer. The author states that the Friday prayer must be performed in a city that has an amir (ruler) and a qadi (judge) who execute judicial decisions and enforce the hudud (fixed penal laws). This is proven by the hadith of Ali (RA) reported by Ibn Abi Shaybah: 'No Friday, no tashriq, no Eid prayer, no Adha except in a congregational metropolis or a great city.'

The passage then elaborates on the definition of 'مصر'. The relied-upon view (zahir al-riwayah) as per Imam al-Sarakhsi is that it must have an amir and a qadi. Some authorities also require a mufti. The definition also includes that the city's buildings be as large as those of Mina. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: some omit the requirement of an amir, requiring only a mufti and a qadi. The author presents two main definitions: one from al-Mukhtasar and another attributed to Abu Hanifa himself, describing a large town with streets, markets, rural districts, and a powerful ruler capable of delivering justice. Al-Bada'i considers this latter definition the most correct.

Special cases are discussed: a villager entering the city on Friday becomes obligated if he intends to stay; a traveler entering without intending to stay is not obligated unless he intends to reside for fifteen days. The passage also addresses the case of a female qadi or ruler. The apparent ruling from Fath al-Qadir is that a town with a female qadi is not a valid metropolis, but the author opines otherwise, citing al-Bada'i that a woman can act as sultan and delegate a male imam for Friday prayer.

Finally, there is a grammatical discussion about the conjunction in the phrase 'enforcing the hudud'—whether it is a conjunction of distinct things or specific to general. The author sides with the latter view for emphasis.

Overall, this passage establishes that Friday prayer requires an urban settlement with executive and judicial authority.

Key Terms
مصر (Misr): A city with an amir and qadi who have the authority to enforce hudud and execute judgments, and with a minimum size of buildings like Mina.
أمير (Amir): A ruler or governor with executive power to enforce law and order.
قاض (Qadi): A judge who has authority to adjudicate disputes and enforce Islamic punishments.
حدود (Hudud): Fixed penal laws prescribed by Shari'ah, such as for theft and adultery.
مفتي (Mufti): A scholar qualified to issue legal opinions (fatwas).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (operative) position in the Hanafi school, what is the minimum requirement for a city to be considered a 'مصر' for Friday prayer?
A) A mufti and a qadi who enforce hudud.
B) An amir and a qadi who enforce hudud and judgments.
C) A population of at least 40 residents.
D) A large building area equal to Mina.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's operative definition of 'مصر' for Friday prayer requires an amir and a qadi who can enforce hudud and judgments.

Traveling on Friday and Obligation of Jumu'ah
Ruling on when a resident may travel on Friday and when a traveler must attend Jumu'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on when a resident may travel on Friday and when a traveler must attend Jumu'ah, based on the timing of the obligation.

Core Idea
Jumu'ah becomes obligatory at the end of Zuhr time, not its beginning – this determines the travel exemption.

In this passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, we learn two related rulings about Jumu'ah and travel. The key is understanding that the obligation of Jumu'ah is deferred to the very end of its time (the time of Zuhr). This has direct consequences for both a resident wanting to travel on Friday and a traveler arriving in a city. The ruling is derived from the nature of the obligation and facilitated by istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage covers two distinct cases: a resident intending to travel on Friday, and a traveler arriving in a city on Friday.
Separate these clearly to avoid confusion.
2
Find the common rationale
Both rulings rely on the same principle: Jumu'ah becomes obligatory only at the end of Zuhr time ( آخر الوقت ).
This is the central legal point.
3
Apply the rationale to each scenario
For the resident: if he leaves the built-up area before the end of Zuhr time, he is a traveler at that moment and Jumu'ah is not obligatory. For the traveler: if he arrives but does not intend to stay 15 days, he remains a traveler and is not obligated.
The built-up area (العمران) marks the boundary of residency.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some other schools hold that Jumu'ah becomes obligatory at the beginning of Zuhr time, so early departure would not exempt.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on different usul.
5
Understand the role of istihsan
The Hanafi ruling uses istihsan (juristic preference) to facilitate travel, overriding a strict analogy that would tie obligation to the start of time.
Istihsan is a key Hanafi principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفقيه : إن نوى الخروج من يومه ذلك وإن كان بعد دخول وقت الجمعة لا تلزمه المصري إذا أراد أن يسافر يوم الجمعة لا بأس به إذا خرج من العمران قبل خروج وقت الظهر لأن الجمعة إنما تجب في آخر الوقت وهو مسافر في آخر الوقت، والمسافر إذا قدم المصر يوم الجمعة على عزم أن لا يخرج يوم الجمعة لا تلزمه الجمعة ما لم ينو الإقامة خمسة عشر يوماً اهـ.
The jurist said: If he intends to leave that day, even if after the entry of Jumu'ah time, it does not become obligatory upon him. For the resident who wants to travel on Friday, there is no harm if he leaves the built-up area before the exit of Zuhr time, because Jumu'ah is only obligatory at the end of its time, and he is a traveler at the end of its time. And if a traveler arrives at the city on Friday with the determination not to leave on Friday, Jumu'ah is not obligatory upon him unless he intends to stay for fifteen days. End.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq states two rulings related to travel and Jumu'ah. First: A resident who intends to travel on Friday may leave the built-up area before Zuhr time ends, because Jumu'ah only becomes binding at the very end of its time (when the imam begins the khutbah). Since he will be outside the city limits at that point, he is considered a traveler and Jumu'ah is not obligatory on him. Second: A traveler who arrives in the city on Friday morning is not required to pray Jumu'ah unless he intends to stay for fifteen days or more (which would make him a resident). The reasoning is the same: at the time of obligation, he is still a traveler. The emphasis is on the time of obligation being the end of Zuhr time, not its beginning.

Key Terms
العمران (al-ʿumrān): The built-up area of the city, beyond which one is considered a traveler.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does the obligation of Jumu'ah become binding?
A) At the beginning of Zuhr time
B) At the end of Zuhr time
C) At the time of the khutbah
D) At the time of the imam's arrival

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school determines the obligation of Jumu'ah at the end of Zuhr time, not the beginning, which governs the rulings on travel exemptions.

The Hanafi Definition of a City (Mīṣr) for Jumu'ah
Definition of a city for Jumu'ah obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of a city for Jumu'ah and identify the essential conditions of a ruler and judge who execute judgments and ḥudūd.

Core Idea
A city for Jumu'ah is defined by the presence of authority, not merely population size.

The validity of Jumu'ah depends on it being held in a proper city (mīṣr). The Hanafi school has a specific definition focused on the existence of a ruler and judge who can enforce Islamic punishments and judgments. In this lesson, we unpack the core definition from Kanz al-Daqāʾiq and its commentary, exploring the conditions and the preferred view among the Hanafi imams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base definition
Locate the statement from Kanz al-Daqāʾiq: 'a place that has a ruler and a judge who executes judgments and establishes the ḥudūd'.
The phrase 'executes judgments and establishes the ḥudūd' is key.
2
Parse the commentaries' refinements
Note how al-Sarakhsī adds the condition of a mufti, while al-Ẓahīriyya omits the ruler but requires a mufti. Understand the range of opinions within the Hanafi school.
These variations still revolve around authority figures.
3
Distinguish the two chosen definitions
Contrast the definition from al-Mukhtaṣar (implied) with Abū Ḥanīfa's definition: a large town with streets, markets, rural districts, and a capable governor. Note which is preferred.
Abū Ḥanīfa's definition is more detailed and includes both physical and administrative features.
4
Apply the definition to a hypothetical town
Given a town with a population of 10,000 but no judge or ruler who can implement ḥudūd, determine if it qualifies as a mīṣr for Jumu'ah.
According to the Hanafi definition, it does not, because the authority condition is missing.
